Finding Moisture After Sleep: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ghusl
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghusl after finding moisture in sleep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ghusl when finding moisture after sleep, identify the intra-madhhab dispute, and apply the condition of priapism.

Core Idea
Finding moisture after sleep: majority obligates ghusl; Abu Yusuf says madhi – the condition of priapism resolves the attribution.

This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about a scenario that many encounter: waking from sleep to find wetness, but not recalling a dream. The two primary students of Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, differ from their teacher on the ruling. Understanding their arguments and how the school ultimately resolves the dispute is essential for proper fiqh application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
Pinpoint exactly what is being disputed: the ruling on finding 'thin water' (balal) after sleep without memory of a wet dream. Is it semen (requiring ghusl) or pre-ejaculate (madhi, not requiring ghusl)?
The passage specifies the moisture is 'thin' (raqiq), which could resemble madhi.
2
State the majority Hanafi position and its evidence
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two') hold that ghusl is obligatory. Their proof is the hadith from Aisha where the Prophet commands ghusl in this exact situation. They also argue by caution (ihtiyat) because sleep can stimulate desire, and the wetness might be semen thinned by air or food.
The hadith is a Sunnah ahad, so its dalalah is zanni, but the imperative indicates wujub in Hanafi usul.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf considers the moisture to be madhi, arguing by qiyas: if madhi does not require ghusl when awake, it should not require it during sleep (a fortiori). He is followed by other Hanafi scholars like Khalaf ibn Ayyub and Abu al-Layth.
The phrase 'aqyas' indicates Abu Yusuf believed his analogy was stronger – but the school rejected it in favour of the direct hadith.
4
Apply the condition of priapism
The passage adds a key condition: if the penis was erect before sleep, the moisture is attributed to madhi (because erection is a cause for pre-ejaculate) and ghusl is not required. If no priapism, the default is that it is semen.
This condition limits the scope of the dispute – it only applies when there was no pre-sleep erection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) منها: (وُجُودُ مَاءٍ رَقِيقِ بَعْدَ الانتباه من النوم) ولم يتذكر احتلاماً، عندهما، خلافاً لأبي يوسف هو يقول: إنه مذي، وأنه لا يوجب الغسل حال اليقظة، فبالأولى عدمه في المنام، وبه أخذ خلف بن أيوب وأبو الليث، لكونه أقيس، ولهما ما روى أبو داود والترمذي عن عائشة قالت: سئل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الرجل يجد البلل ولا يذكر احتلاماً قال: يغتسل. ولأن النوم مظنة الاحتلام، لأنه راحة فيهيج الشهوة، فيحال عليه، ثم يحتمل أنه كان منياً، فرق بواسطة الهواء أو الغذاء، والاحتياط لازم في باب العبادات. وهذا ( إذا لم يكن ذكره منتشراً قبل النوم)؛ لأن الانتشار قبل النوم سبب لخروج المذي، فيحال عليه، ولو وجد الزوجان، بينهما ماء دون تذكر ومميز بأن لم يظهر غلظه، ولا رقته، ولا بياضه ولا صفرته، ولا طوله، ولا عرضه، يجب عليهما الغسل، في الصحيح احتياطاً
And among them: finding thin water after waking from sleep and not remembering a wet dream, according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf who says it is pre-ejaculate and does not necessitate ghusl when awake, so even more so during sleep. Khalaf ibn Ayyub and Abu al-Layth followed him because it is more analogical. Their evidence is what Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi narrated from Aisha: the Messenger of Allah was asked about a man who finds wetness and does not remember a wet dream, he said: 'He should perform ghusl.' And because sleep is a place for wet dreams, as it is rest that stirs desire, so it is attributed to it. Then it is possible that it was semen, thinned by air or food, and caution is necessary in matters of worship. This is when his penis was not erect before sleep; because erection before sleep is a cause for the exit of pre-ejaculate, so it is attributed to it. And if a married couple finds moisture between them without recollection and indistinguishable (not thick, not thin, not white, not yellow, not long, not wide), then both must wash, in the correct view as caution.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute within the commentary. The issue is whether finding wetness after sleep without remembering a dream necessitates ghusl. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the majority say yes, based on the hadith from Aisha where the Prophet commanded ghusl in this situation. They argue that sleep is a context where desire can stir, so the wetness is presumed to be semen. Caution in worship dictates ghusl. Abu Yusuf disagrees, saying the wetness is madhi (pre-ejaculate) and does not require ghusl, even less so in sleep. He is followed by Khalaf ibn Ayyub and Abu al-Layth, who consider this view more analogical (qiyas). However, the relied-upon position is the majority view. The passage also clarifies that if the penis was erect before sleep, the wetness is attributed to madhi (due to the stimulus of desire) and ghusl is not required. Additionally, if a couple finds moisture between them and cannot distinguish its nature, they must both perform ghusl as a precaution.

Key Terms
مذي (madhy): Thin white fluid that exits from a man when playing with his wife; pre-ejaculate.
بلل (balal): Moisture or wetness, specifically after sleep, that may or may not be semen.
احتلام (ihtilām): A wet dream; experiencing an erotic dream resulting in emission of semen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling if a person finds moisture after sleep without remembering a dream and without priapism before sleep?
A) Ghusl is not required because it is madhi
B) Ghusl is obligatory
C) Only wudu is required
D) No action needed

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, finding moisture after sleep without dream recollection obligates ghusl unless there was priapism before sleep, based on the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) which is the mufta bihi.

Additional Ghusl Causes: Unconsciousness, Menstruation, and Pre-Islam Events
Lists further ghusl causes and discusses obligation even if they occurred before Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three additional causes of obligatory ghusl and explain why ghusl is required even if they occurred before Islam.

Core Idea
Ghusl is obligatory for menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and post-intoxication/fainting moisture, even if pre-conversion.

This passage continues the enumeration of ghusl causes, covering three situations: finding moisture after intoxication or fainting, menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and the ruling that these causes obligate ghusl even if they occurred before embracing Islam. The author resolves an internal Hanafi dispute on when ghusl becomes obligatory for hayd/nifas and supports his reasoning with Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three additional causes
List: (a) finding moisture after intoxication/fainting, (b) menstruation/postpartum bleeding, (c) previous occurrence before Islam.
The first is precautionary; the second includes a dispute; the third is a general principle.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on menstruation
Scholars of Bukhara say ghusl is obligatory only at cessation of blood; al-Karkhi and Iraqis say it is due to the exit of blood, but performed after cessation. The author favors the latter because cessation is purification and cannot cause purification.
Practical outcome is the same: ghusl after bleeding stops.
3
Analyze the dalil for ghusl before Islam
The Quranic verses (5:6 and 2:222) are cited. Verse 2:222 'until they are purified' means 'until they perform ghusl', showing that the state of janabah persists and must be removed for prayer and marital relations.
The condition of purification cannot be fulfilled without ghusl, so it becomes obligatory even for pre-conversion events.
4
Note the consensus on postpartum bleeding
The text states that ghusl from postpartum bleeding is obligatory by consensus (ijma).
No dispute among Hanafis on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) منها: (وُجُودُ بَلَلٍ، ظَنَّهُ مَنِيَا، بَعْدَ إِفَاقَتِهِ مِنْ سُكْرٍ، وَ) بعد إفاقته من (إغماء)، احتياطاً. (و) منها: أنه يفترض الغسل (بِحَيْض، ونفاس)، أي بخروج دم حيض، أو نفاس إلى فرجها الخارج. وقيل: لا يصح أن يُؤولا بخروج الدم، لأنه لا يجب بخروجه الغسل، وإنما يجب عند انقطاعه وهو اختيار مشايخ بخارى. وفيه نظر، إذ الانقطاع طهارة، ويستحيل أن توجب الطهارة طهارة، وإنما يوجبها الخارج النجس، وهو اختيار الكرخي، وعامة العراقيين. وإنما لا يغتسل قبل الانقطاع لعدم الفائدة باستمرار الدم لا لأنه لا يرفع الحدث المتقدم. وحاصله: أن الحيض، أو النفاس موجب بشرط الانقطاع، والتحقيق أنه سبب للاتصاف بالحدث، وانقطاعه شرط لتبقى طهارة الاغتسال وسبب وجوبه إرادة أو وجوب ما لا يحل معه، كما تقدم. (و) يفترض الاغتسال في جميع موجباته، ولو حصلت الأشياء المذكورة قبل الإسلام، في الأصح؛ لبقاء صفة الجنابة ونحوها بعد الإسلام، ولا يمكن أداء المشروط بزوال الجنابة، وما في معناها إلا به، فيفترض عليه لكونه مسلماً مكلفاً بالطهارة عند إرادة الصلاة ونحوها، لقوله تعالى: {يا أيها الذين آمنوا إذا قمتم إلى الصلاة} [المائدة: 6] وقوله تعالى: {ولا تقربوهن حتى يطهرن} [البقرة: 222] بالتشديد أي يغتسلن، فلولا أن الغسل فرض، لما منع عن حقه، وهو الوطء، إلى غاية الغسل، وحرم عليها تمكينه ضرورة، فإذا انقطع وجب عليها تمكينه من حقه، ولا تتوصل إليه إلا بالاغتسال، فيجب كوجوبه، ووجوب الاغتسال فيما دون العشرة، يلزمه فيها أيضاً، بدلالة النص، لأن وجوب الغسل باعتبار الخروج عن الحيض، وقد وجد، وافتراض الاغتسال من النفاس بالإجماع.
And among them: finding moisture which he thinks is semen after recovering from intoxication, and after recovering from fainting, as a precaution. And among them: that ghusl becomes obligatory due to menstruation and postpartum bleeding, i.e., by the exit of menstrual blood or postpartum blood to the external vagina. And it is said: it is not correct to attribute it to the exit of blood, because ghusl does not become obligatory by its exit; rather it becomes obligatory at its cessation. This is the choice of the scholars of Bukhara. But this is questionable, because cessation is purification, and it is impossible for purification to necessitate purification; rather it is necessitated by the impure exiting, which is the choice of al-Karkhi and the majority of Iraqis. The reason one does not perform ghusl before cessation is not because it does not remove the prior state of impurity, but because there is no benefit due to continuous blood. The conclusion is that menstruation or postpartum causes (obligation) with the condition of cessation. The reality is that it is a cause for being described as impure, and its cessation is a condition for the purity of the ghusl, and the cause of its obligation is the intention or obligation of what is not permissible with it, as previously mentioned. And ghusl is obligatory in all its causes, even if the mentioned things occurred before Islam, according to the correct view, because the state of janabah and similar remains after Islam, and the condition cannot be fulfilled without removal of janabah. And what is equivalent to it except by it, so it becomes obligatory upon him because he is a Muslim obligated with purity when intending prayer and the like. For the saying of Allah: 'O you who have believed, when you rise to prayer' [5:6] and His saying: 'And do not approach them until they are purified' [2:222] meaning they perform ghusl. So if ghusl were not obligatory, he would not have been prevented from his right, which is intercourse, until the end of ghusl, and she is forbidden to allow him out of necessity. So when it ceases, she must allow him his right, and she cannot achieve that except by ghusl, so it becomes obligatory like it is obligatory. And the obligation of ghusl for the menstrual period within ten days is also binding, by textual indication, because the obligation of ghusl is based on emerging from menstruation, and that has occurred. And the obligation of ghusl from postpartum bleeding is by consensus.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of ghusl causes. It covers three things: (1) finding moisture after intoxication or fainting, which requires ghusl as a precaution since one might have had an emission; (2) menstruation and postpartum bleeding, where there is a dispute among Hanafi scholars: the scholars of Bukhara say ghusl becomes obligatory only at the cessation of blood, while al-Karkhi and the Iraqis say it is the exit of blood that causes the obligation (though ghusl is performed after cessation). The author favors the latter view because cessation is purification itself, and a purification cannot cause another purification; rather, the impure exiting causes it. The practical outcome is the same: ghusl is performed after blood stops. (3) If any of these causes occurred before embracing Islam, ghusl is still obligatory after conversion because the state of janabah or similar persists. The Quranic verses (5:6 and 2:222) are cited to show the obligation of ghusl for the menstruating woman to enable prayer and marital relations.

Key Terms
حَيْض (hayḍ): Menstrual bleeding that occurs from the uterus of a woman at healthy intervals.
نفاس (nifās): Postpartum bleeding that occurs after childbirth.
سكر (sukr): Intoxication, a state where a person loses control and may not remember events.
إغماء (ighmā’): Fainting or unconsciousness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person experiences a cause of ghusl (like janabah) before embracing Islam, what is the ruling after he becomes Muslim?
A) He does not need to perform ghusl because the previous state is nullified by Islam
B) He must perform ghusl because the state of impurity persists
C) He only needs to perform wudu
D) It is recommended but not obligatory

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is obligatory for these causes even if they preceded Islam, because the state of impurity remains until removed.

Washing the Deceased: A Communal Obligation
States the ruling and its category

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for washing the deceased Muslim and explain the meaning of fard kifayah in this context.

Core Idea
Washing the dead Muslim is fard kifayah – enough if some do it.

Death is a reality every community faces. Islam prescribes specific rites, beginning with washing the body (ghusl al-mayyit). This brief ruling establishes that the duty rests on the community as a whole, not on every individual. Understanding the distinction between fard kifayah and fard ayn is essential for correctly fulfilling this obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The text states 'washing the dead Muslim is obligatory' (yuf'taradu taghsīl al-mayyit). The verb 'yuf'taradu' indicates a fard (obligation).
Note that the ruling uses the passive to highlight the act, not the performer.
2
Understand the Qualification: 'Kifayah'
The parenthetical 'kifayah' qualifies the obligation: it is a communal duty, not an individual one. If a sufficient number of Muslims perform the washing, the obligation is discharged for all.
The term 'kifayah' literally means 'sufficiency'.
3
Contrast with Fard 'Ayn
Fard kifayah differs from fard ayn (personal obligation) such as the five daily prayers. For a fard kifayah, if some fulfill it, the rest are absolved; if none perform it, the entire community is sinful.
4
Locate the Ruling in the Fiqh Structure
The author promises to detail the washing procedure later ('in its place'). This sentence serves as an introductory anchor, categorizing the act before explaining its method.
This is typical of matn texts: first establish the hukm, then elaborate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُفترضُ تَغْسِيلُ المَيِّتِ المسلم (كفاية)، وسنذكره في محله إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it is obligatory to wash the dead Muslim, as a communal obligation, and we will mention it in its place, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This is a brief ruling establishing that ghusl of the deceased for a Muslim is a fard kifayah (communal obligation). If some Muslims perform it, the obligation is discharged for all. The author promises to detail this ruling later in the appropriate section (funeral prayers). The statement serves as a heading and anchors the rule in the reader's mind.

Key Terms
تغسيل الميت (taghsīl al-mayyit): The act of washing the deceased body according to Islamic funerary rites.
كفاية (kifāyah): Sufficient; meaning a communal obligation (fard kifayah) where if some perform it, others are absolved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal classification of washing the deceased Muslim according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fard ayn (individual obligation)
B) Fard kifayah (communal obligation)
C) Sunnah muakkadah
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Washing a deceased Muslim is a communal obligation (fard kifayah); the duty is fulfilled when some Muslims perform it, and the entire community is absolved.

Ruling on Washing the Deceased as Fard Kifayah
States the ruling and provides Quranic proof for washing the deceased.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing the deceased as fard kifayah and explain the Quranic proof and analogy used.

Core Idea
Washing the deceased is a communal obligation; the Quranic command for purification applies by analogy.

In this passage, the author establishes the legal ruling for washing the deceased. He does not simply assert the ruling; he anchors it in Quranic evidence and rational analogy, showing how purification obligations apply even after death. Understanding this proof structure is key to appreciating the Hanafi method of deriving fiqh from primary sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? Washing the deceased (taghsil). What is the hukm level? Fard kifayah, meaning a collective obligation.
Fard kifayah differs from fard 'ayn: if some perform it, the rest are absolved.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two Quranic verses: Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6 (about wudu and purification for prayer) and Surah al-Baqarah 2:222 (about purification after menstruation).
Both verses use imperative forms that indicate obligation in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How do these verses prove the obligation of washing the deceased? The author argues by analogy: if purification is obligatory for the living for acts like prayer, and the deceased is a Muslim who was required to be pure, then the deceased must be purified. Since the deceased cannot do it themselves, the obligation falls on the living community.
This analogy (qiyas) is based on the shared illa of the need for purification for burial.
4
Note the supporting reasoning
The author also mentions that ghusl for menstruation and postpartum bleeding is obligatory by consensus (ijma). This reinforces the principle that ghusl is a binding act.
Consensus strengthens the argument for the obligation of ghusl in general.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that washing the Muslim deceased is fard kifayah. This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.
No intra-Hanafi disagreement is noted here; it is a settled ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفترض تغسيل الميت، كفاية. معناها إلا به، فيفترض عليه لكونه مسلماً مكلفاً بالطهارة عند إرادة الصلاة ونحوها، لقوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا قُمْتُمْ إِلَى الصَّلَاةِ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٢] بالتشديد أي يغتسلن، فلولا أن الغسل فرض، لَمَا مُنِعَ عن حقه، وهو الوطء، إلى غاية الغسل، وحرم عليها تمكينه ضرورة، فإذا انقطع وجب عليها تمكينه من حقه، ولا تتوصل إليه إلا بالاغتسال، فيجب كوجوبه، ووجوب الاغتسال فيما دون العشرة، يلزمه فيها أيضاً، بدلالة النص، لأن وجوب الغسل باعتبار الخروج عن الحيض، وقد وجد، وافتراض الاغتسال من النفاس بالإجماع. ويُفترضُ تَغْسِيلُ المَيِّتِ المسلم كفاية، وسنذكره في محله إن شاء الله تعالى.
And washing the deceased is an obligation, a sufficiency. Its meaning: the obligation of it is [established] because he is a Muslim required to be pure when intending prayer and similar acts, due to His statement: 'O you who have believed, when you rise to prayer...' and His statement: 'And do not approach them until they are pure' with tashdid, i.e., until they perform ghusl. If ghusl were not obligatory, he would not have been prevented from his right, which is intercourse, until the time of ghusl, and it would have been impermissible for her to allow it necessarily. So when it ceases, she must allow him his right, and she cannot achieve that except by performing ghusl, so it becomes obligatory like his [right]. And the obligation of ghusl within ten days is incumbent upon her also, by implication of the text, because the obligation of ghusl is due to leaving the state of menstruation, and that has occurred. And the obligation of ghusl from postpartum bleeding is by consensus. And washing the Muslim deceased is an obligation, a sufficiency, and we will mention it in its place, God willing.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the obligation of washing the deceased. The author begins by stating that washing the deceased is a fard kifayah (communal obligation). He then explains the reasoning: since every Muslim is obligated to perform purification (taharah) for prayer and similar acts, the deceased, being a Muslim, is also subject to this obligation. However, because the deceased cannot perform the purification himself, it devolves upon the living community as a collective duty. He cites Quranic verses: Surah al-Ma'idah (5:6) regarding wudu and ghusl, and Surah al-Baqarah (2:222) regarding the prohibition of intercourse until a menstruating woman becomes pure (i.e., performs ghusl). The author reasons that if ghusl were not obligatory, the prohibition would not be meaningful; the fact that it is a barrier to intercourse shows it is a required act. Similarly, he notes that ghusl for postpartum bleeding is obligatory by ijma (consensus). Then he reiterates the ruling: washing the Muslim deceased is fard kifayah, and promises to discuss its details later in the chapter on funerals. Note that 'fard kifayah' means that if some members of the community perform it, the obligation is lifted from all; if none do, all sin.

Key Terms
تغسيل (taghsil): The act of washing the deceased body.
كفاية (kifayah): A collective obligation; sufficient if performed by some individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, washing the deceased Muslim is classified as what type of obligation?
A) Fard 'ayn
B) Fard kifayah
C) Wajib
D) Sunnah mu'akkadah

Key Takeaway
Washing the Muslim deceased is a communal obligation (fard kifayah) established by Quranic verses on purification and analogy; if not performed by some, the whole community is sinful.

Ten Cases Where Ghusl Is Not Required
Lists and defines ten cases where ghusl is not obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten cases where ghusl is not required and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding childbirth without blood.

Core Idea
Ghusl is not required for madhi, wady, wet dream without emission, and seven other cases because the full cause of janabah is absent.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational chapter from Nur al-Idah that enumerates ten specific situations where ghusl (ritual bathing) is not obligatory. While many students assume that any emission from the private parts necessitates ghusl, the Hanafi school—supported by scholarly consensus and reasoned analogy—identifies clear exceptions. Understanding these cases helps refine your grasp of the causes of janabah and the precise boundaries of ritual purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify each item and its ruling
Read through the ten items and note that for each, ghusl is not required. The list includes madhi, wady, wet dream without wetness, childbirth without blood, insertion with a barrier, enema, finger insertion, intercourse with an animal or dead woman without ejaculation.
Count carefully; the text says 'ten' but lists nine explicitly; the tenth may be implied as 'any similar case without full pleasure/emission'.
2
Analyze madhi and wady definitions
Focus on the linguistic and legal definitions of madhi (thin fluid at desire) and wady (thick fluid after urination). Note the consensus (ijma) that ghusl is not required for these.
The author gives three pronunciations for each; the lightest is most eloquent.
3
Examine the wet dream case
Understand that ihtilam without wetness does not require ghusl for both men and women, based on the hadith of Umm Sulaym.
The woman is treated like the man in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
4
Explore the childbirth dispute
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa says ghusl is obligatory as a precaution; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no ghusl (the soundest view). Understand the reasoning (nifas vs. precaution).
The text marks the Sahibayn's view as 'fi al-sahih' (the soundest).
5
Review the remaining cases
For each of the remaining cases (barrier, enema, finger insertion, bestiality/dead woman), note the rationale: absence of complete pleasure or ejaculation.
The commentator of al-Munyah suggests ghusl is required for finger insertion in the vagina due to predominance of desire, but the chosen view in this text is no ghusl.

Chapter: Ten things from which ghusl is not performed: madhi, wady, wet dream without wetness, childbirth without seeing blood after it – according to the soundest view – insertion with a cloth preventing feeling pleasure, enema, insertion of a finger and the like into either of the two passages, intercourse with an animal or a dead woman without ejaculation. Ten things from which ghusl is not performed: madhi, which is a thin white fluid emitted at the time of desire, not due to desire, and without gushing, and not followed by lethargy; one may not feel its exit; it is more common in women than men. On the women's side it is called 'qudhi'. For madhi there are three pronunciations: with sukun on the dhal and tashdid on the ya, and with kasr on the dhal and shaddah on the ya – these two are well-known, but the lighter pronunciation is more eloquent and common; the third is with kasr on the dhal and sukun on the ya. It is said: madhi with tashdid and amdha and mudhi with tashdid; the first is more eloquent. And from them: wady, which is a thick white turbid fluid resembling semen in thickness and differing in turbidity; it has no smell; it comes after urination when the nature is constipated, and when carrying something heavy; it may precede urine; it exits a drop or two or the like. The scholars have agreed that ghusl is not obligatory due to emission of madhi and wady. Its pronunciation is with sukun on the important dhal and tashdid on the ya; according to the majority of linguists, nothing else is permissible. It is said: wady with tashdid on the dhal and tashdid on the ya? Actually it is said: wady with tashdid and awda and waddi with tashdid; the first is more eloquent. And from them: wet dream without wetness; a woman is like a man in the apparent narration, based on the hadith of Umm Sulaym we presented. And from them: childbirth without seeing blood after it – according to the soundest view; this is the later position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because ghusl is attached to nifas, and nifas did not truly occur; wudu is obligatory upon her due to the moisture present from childbirth. Abu Hanifa said: ghusl is upon her even if she does not see blood, as a precaution, because she is not free from a little blood apparently, as previously mentioned. And from them: insertion with a cloth preventing the existence of pleasure – this is according to the most correct view; we previously mentioned the necessity of ghusl due to it as a precaution. And from them: enema; because it is for expelling wastes, not for fulfilling desire. And from them: insertion of a finger and the like, such as a manufactured likeness of a penis made of leather or wood, into either of the two passages, according to the chosen view, due to lack of desire, like intercourse with animals. The commentator of al-Munyah said: the preferred view is to obligate ghusl for inserting a finger into the woman's vagina due to predominance of desire, but not the anus – this is a research of his. And from them: intercourse with an animal or a dead woman without ejaculation; because its cause is incomplete, and ejaculation is not predominant here to take its place.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from a chapter on ten cases where ghusl (ritual bathing) is not required. The author lists each case, often providing definitions and linguistic notes. The ten items are: (1) madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid), (2) wady (post-urine discharge), (3) wet dream without emission, (4) childbirth without subsequent blood, (5) penetration with a barrier preventing pleasure, (6) enema, (7) insertion of a finger or similar into either orifice, (8) bestiality without ejaculation, (9) intercourse with a dead woman without ejaculation, (10) and others? Actually the initial list includes nine, but the text says ten; counting carefully: madhi, wady, ihtilam bila balal, wilada, ilaj bi khirqa, huqna, idkhal isba', wat' bahima, wat' mayyita = nine? But the text explicitly says 'ashara' so there is one more perhaps implied as a category. In the detailed explanation, he covers each. For madhi and wady, he cites consensus (ijma) that ghusl is not required. For wet dream without wetness, the woman is like the man (no ghusl). For childbirth without blood, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says ghusl is precautionary, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no ghusl (the soundest view). The remaining items are based on lack of complete sexual pleasure or ejaculation. The passage also discusses linguistic variations of terms. Overall, the ruling is that ghusl is not obligatory in these cases because the cause of janabah (e.g., ejaculation or full penetration with pleasure) is absent.

Key Terms
مذي (madhi): A thin white fluid emitted at the time of desire, without gushing or subsequent lethargy.
ودي (wady): A thick white turbid fluid discharged after urination or due to heavy lifting.
احتلام (ihtilam): The state of having a wet dream; here specifically without actual emission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She must perform ghusl according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
B) She must perform ghusl according to Abu Hanifa but not according to the Sahibayn.
C) She does not need ghusl according to any Hanafi scholar.
D) She needs both ghusl and wudu.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is only required when janabah is fully realized: either through ejaculation (men/women) or through full penetration with no barrier and presence of pleasure.

Ruling on Madhi and Wadi: No Ghusl Required
Defines madhi and wadi and states the consensus that they do not require ghusl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that madhi and wadi do not require ghusl, explain the basis of ijma, and distinguish these fluids from mani.

Core Idea
Madhi and wadi are not seminal fluids; ghusl is not required — only washing the affected area suffices.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling on two types of fluids that are often misunderstood. Unlike mani (semen), which necessitates ghusl, madhi (pre-ejaculate) and wadi (post-urine discharge) are ruled as not requiring a full bath. The lesson provides a clear definition and the consensus of scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fluids
Read the definitions of madhi and wadi carefully. Note their physical characteristics, when they occur, and their prevalence in women.
Madhi is thin, white, emerges with desire often without sensation; wadi is thick, turbid, after urination.
2
State the ruling
From the text: ghusl is not obligatory (la yajibu al-ghusl) for the exit of madhi and wadi.
This is the core ruling.
3
Locate the evidence
The author cites ijma (scholarly consensus): 'And the scholars have agreed that ghusl is not obligatory due to the exit of madhi and wadi.'
Ijma is a primary source of Islamic law.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, if madhi or wadi exits, one only needs to wash the area of contact; no ghusl is needed. Wudu may be invalidated (though not mentioned here, but known from other texts).
This contrasts with mani, which requires ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وَ) منها: (وَدْي)، وهو : ماء أبيض كدر ثخين يشبه المني في الثخانة، ويخالفه في الكدرة، ولا رائحة له، ويخرج عقب البول، إذا كانت الطبيعة مستمسكة . وعند حمل شيء ثقيل، وقد يسبق البول، ويخرج قطرة أو قطرتين، أو نحوهما، وأجمع العلماء على أنه لا يجب الغسل بخروج المذي والودي، وهو بإسكان الدال المهملة، وتخفيف الياء، ولا يجوز عند جمهور أهل اللغة غير هذا، يقال: ودي بتخفيف الدال، وأودي وودي بالتشديد والأول أفصح

He then states the ruling that ghusl is not required for either, supported by scholarly consensus (ijma). The Hanafi position is established: madhi and wadi are not full sexual fluids and only require washing of the affected area, not a full bath.

الودي (al-wadi): A thick turbid white fluid that emerges after urination or due to heavy lifting; it has no odor.

Wet Dream Without Discharge – No Ghusl Obligatory
States ruling on wet dream without discharge, including women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for a wet dream without discharge and explain the proof from the hadith of Umm Sulaym.

Core Idea
Ghusl is only required after a wet dream if there is actual discharge – the dream alone is not sufficient.

This lesson examines a specific item from the list of actions that break wudu but do NOT require ghusl: a wet dream (ihtilam) without any discharge. The text clarifies that neither men nor women need to perform ghusl if they awaken to find no wetness. The ruling is based on the well-known hadith of Umm Sulaym (may Allah be pleased with her), which establishes that ghusl is linked to the emission of fluid, not merely the dream experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a wet dream without any discharge. The ruling is that it does not necessitate ghusl.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Umm Sulaym, which is a proof from the Sunnah.
The hadith is mentioned earlier in the text; we rely on its established meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith indicates that ghusl is only required when there is emission; without it, no ghusl.
4
Note the scope
The ruling applies equally to men and women as per the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
5
Consider the authority
This is the position of the Hanafi school in the zahir al-riwaya, meaning it is the relied-upon view.
There may be an alternate narration, but the fatwa is based on this.

Childbirth Without Blood: Do You Need Ghusl? An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Presents the rule and an intra-Hanafi dispute on ghusl after childbirth without blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on ghusl after childbirth without blood and identify the mufta bihi (operative) position.

Core Idea
Ghusl after childbirth is linked to blood; if no blood, the later Hanafi view says no ghusl.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: a woman gives birth but sees no postpartum blood. Is ghusl required? The answer differs between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Understanding this dispute teaches us how the Hanafi school weighs textual cause (illah) against precaution (ihtiyat).

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify Abu Hanifa's view (ghusl wajib precautionarily) and the Sahibayn's view (no ghusl).
The Sahibayn's later view is the mufta bihi.
2
Identify the legal cause (illah)
Recognise that ghusl is attached to nifas (postpartum bleeding).
Nifas is the defined cause for obligation of ghusl after childbirth.
3
Apply the cause to the case
Since no blood is seen, the cause is absent, so according to the Sahibayn, no ghusl.
Abu Hanifa argues precautionarily that blood is likely present but unseen.
4
Analyse the precautionary argument (ihtiyat)
Abu Hanifa's view is based on the assumption that a small amount of blood is typically present.
The later school rejects this as a basis for obligation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author states 'in the correct view', siding with the Sahibayn.
This establishes the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) منها: (ولادةٌ مِنْ غَيْرِ رُؤيَةِ دَمِ بَعْدَهَا، في الصحيح)، وهو قول أبي يوسف ومحمد آخراً، لتعلق الغسل بالنفاس، ولم يوجد حقيقة، والوضوء لازم عليها للرطوبة الموجودة بالولادة. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: عليها الغسل، وإن لم تـر دمـاً احتياطاً؛ لعدم خلوها عن قليل دم ظاهراً كما تقدم.
(And) from them: (Childbirth without seeing blood after it, in the correct view) - and this is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad later, because ghusl is attached to postpartum bleeding (nifas), and it has not been found in reality; and wudu is necessary for her due to the moisture present during childbirth. And Abu Hanifa said: Ghusl is upon her even if she does not see blood, out of precaution; because she is not free from a small amount of blood apparently as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage deals with the fourth item: a woman who gives birth but sees no postpartum blood afterward. The author states that the correct view (as-sahih) is that no ghusl is required, which is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad later in their scholarship. Their reasoning is that ghusl after childbirth is linked to nifas (postpartum bleeding); if no blood is seen, the cause for ghusl is absent. However, wudu is still required due to the moisture of childbirth. Abu Hanifa disagrees, holding that ghusl is obligatory as a precaution, because there is usually a small amount of blood that may not be seen. The text thus presents a clear intra-Hanafi dispute.

Key Terms
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding; the cause for ghusl after childbirth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), is ghusl required for a woman who gives birth without seeing any postpartum blood?
A) Yes, ghusl is always required after childbirth.
B) No, ghusl is not required because the cause (nifas) is absent.
C) Only if she experiences pain.
D) Ghusl is required but only after 40 days.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl after childbirth is obligatory only if postpartum blood (nifas) is present; if no blood is seen, the correct Hanafi view is that ghusl is not required.

Insertion with Cloth Preventing Pleasure: No Ghusl
Ruling statement: no ghusl with barrier preventing pleasure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl when a barrier prevents pleasure and identify the precautionary alternative.

Core Idea
If a barrier prevents pleasure during insertion, ghusl is not required.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario regarding the obligation of ghusl after intercourse. The classical Hanafi texts discuss whether full insertion alone suffices to make ghusl obligatory, or whether the experience of pleasure is a necessary element. The passage states that with a cloth barrier that blocks pleasure, the most correct opinion (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that ghusl is not obligatory, though a precautionary view recommends it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon (insertion with a cloth barrier) and what the ruling is (no ghusl required, according to the most correct view).
2
Understand the basis
The ruling hinges on the absence of pleasure due to the barrier. In Hanafi fiqh, ghusl is linked to the possibility of pleasure.
3
Consider the precautionary view
Recognize that a minority view recommends ghusl as a precaution (iḥtiyāṭ), but the relied-upon position is no ghusl.

Source Text

(And) from them: (Insertion with a cloth that prevents the existence of pleasure) - this is the most correct view. And we have presented the obligation of ghusl due to it precautionarily.

Enema: No Ghusl Required
States that an enema does not require ghusl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl after an enema and explain the underlying reason based on the intent of the act.

Core Idea
An enema does not require ghusl because its purpose is medical, not sexual.

In this lesson, we examine a short but important ruling from Nur al-Idah: administering an enema does not necessitate ghusl. While many acts involving the private parts trigger major impurity, this one is explicitly excluded due to its medical nature. Understanding this exception helps clarify the principle that ghusl is tied to sexual desire or emission, not to any insertion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is an enema (al-huqnah). The ruling is that it does not require ghusl.
2
Identify the reason (illah)
The text gives the reason: because it is for expelling waste, not for satisfying desire.
The illah (legal cause) is the absence of sexual intent.
3
Trace the principle behind the ruling
Ghusl is required for acts linked to sexual desire or emission. Medical acts are not linked, so no ghusl.
4
Compare with other acts that do require ghusl
For example, sexual intercourse or ejaculation require ghusl because they involve desire or emission.
This highlights the distinction based on intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) منها: (حقْنَةٌ)؛ لأنها لإخراج الفضلات، لا لقضاء الشهوة.
(And) from them: (Enema) because it is for expelling waste, not for satisfying desire.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This short passage covers the sixth item: an enema. The reason given is that an enema is a medical procedure to expel waste matter, not a means for sexual gratification. Therefore, it does not require ghusl. The underlying principle is that ghusl is connected to sexual activity or emission; enemas have no connection to desire, so they remain outside the scope of ghusl obligation.

Key Terms
الحقنة (al-huqnah): An enema; injection of fluid into the rectum for medical purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does administering an enema require ghusl according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because something enters the body.
B) No, because it is for expelling waste, not for pleasure.
C) Only if it causes ejaculation.
D) Only if the enema fluid is liquid.

Key Takeaway
An enema does not require ghusl because it is for medical purposes, not for sexual gratification.

Inserting Finger or Similar into Private Parts: No Ghusl (Chosen View)
Ruling on a cause of ritual impurity with intra-madhhab discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for inserting a finger or similar object into the private parts and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the vagina.

Core Idea
Inserting a finger does not require ghusl; desire is deficient, like bestiality.

The seventh nullifier of wudu (one of the causes of ritual impurity) is inserting a finger or similar object into either the front or back passage. The chosen Hanafi position is that ghusl is not required because the level of desire is low, analogous to bestiality. However, the commentator of al-Minya offers a nuanced view for vaginal insertion due to overwhelming desire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is inserting a finger or similar object (like an artificial penis) into one of the two private parts (front or back). The chosen view states that no ghusl is required.
2
Understand the reasoning (qiyas)
The ruling is based on analogy (qiyas) to bestiality, where no ghusl is required unless ejaculation occurs. The 'illa (effective cause) is deficient desire – inserting a finger does not involve the full desire of intercourse.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
The commentator of al-Minya (a later Hanafi scholar) argued that inserting a finger into a woman's vagina often involves overwhelming desire, so ghusl should be required for that case, but not for the anus.
This is his own research (bahth), not the adopted view.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that no ghusl is required for any finger insertion. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
The text explicitly says 'alā al-mukhtār' (on the chosen view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) منها : ( إدخالُ إصْبَعِ ونَحوه ) ، كشـبـه ذكـر مصنـوع مـن جـلـد، أو خشب ( في أحد السَّبِيلَين)، على المختار، لقصور الشهوة، كإيتان البهائم. وقال شارح « المنـيـة » : الأولى إيجاب الغسل بإدخال الإصبع في قبل المرأة، لغلبة الشهوة دون الدبر، وهو بحث منه.
(And) from them: (Inserting a finger and the like) such as an artificial penis made of leather or wood (into one of the two passages), according to the chosen view, because of the deficiency of desire, like approaching animals. And the commentator of al-Minya said: The better view is to require ghusl for inserting a finger into the woman's vagina, due to the overwhelming desire, not the anus; and this is a discussion from him.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
The seventh item is inserting a finger or similar object (like a dildo) into either the front or back passage. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that ghusl is not required, because the level of desire is low, analogous to bestiality (which also does not require ghusl without ejaculation). However, the commentator of al-Minya provides a dissenting view: he considers that inserting a finger into the vagina often involves strong desire, so ghusl should be required for that, but not for the anus. This is his own research (bahth). The text highlights that the main position is no ghusl, but there is a notable alternate view.

Key Terms
السبيلين (al-sabīlayn): The two passages: the front (vagina/penis) and the back (anus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in the Hanafi school, which of the following requires ghusl?
A) Inserting a finger into the anus.
B) Inserting a finger into the vagina.
C) Inserting a finger into the mouth.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Inserting a finger or similar object into the private parts does not require ghusl in the Hanafi school, by analogy to bestiality due to deficient desire.

Intercourse with Animal or Dead Woman Without Ejaculation: No Ghusl
Ruling on ghusl for intercourse with animal or dead woman without ejaculation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for intercourse with an animal or dead woman without ejaculation and explain the legal reasoning based on the incomplete cause (sabab).

Core Idea
Without ejaculation, ghusl is not required because the cause is incomplete.

The text addresses specific cases of sexual intercourse with a dead woman or an animal. In Hanafi fiqh, ghusl becomes wajib either by actually producing ejaculation (inzal) or by full insertion (ilaj) when it normally leads to ejaculation. In these abnormal contexts, ejaculation is not typical, so insertion alone does not trigger the obligation of ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is intercourse with an animal or dead woman without ejaculation. Ruling: ghusl is not required.
2
Understand the cause of ghusl
The cause (sabab) for ghusl is either ejaculation or insertion that usually leads to ejaculation.
In normal intercourse, insertion stands in for ejaculation.
3
Apply the cause to the case
In these cases, ejaculation is not prevalent, so insertion alone does not complete the cause.
4
Note the talil
The reasoning is that the cause is incomplete (lam yakun kamil al-sabab).
The text says 'li'adam kamil sababihi'.
5
Contrast with normal intercourse
In normal intercourse with a living woman, insertion substitutes for ejaculation because it typically leads to it.
This contrast highlights why the ruling differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) منها: (وَطْهُ بهيمةٍ (أو امرأة (مَيْتَةٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ انْزَال)؛ لعدم كمال سببه، وليس الإنزال غالباً هنــا لـيقــام سببه، وهو: الإيلاج مقام الإنزال،
(And) from them: (Intercourse with an animal or a dead woman without ejaculation); because its cause is incomplete, and ejaculation is not prevalent here to take the place of the cause, which is insertion taking the place of ejaculation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
The eighth item deals with sexual intercourse with an animal or a dead body (woman) without ejaculation. The reasoning is that the cause (sabab) for ghusl is not fully present. Usually, ghusl becomes obligatory either by actual ejaculation or by insertion (ilaj) which stands in for the cause of ghusl. In these cases, ejaculation is not typical, and insertion alone does not suffice to trigger ghusl because the context lacks the usual desire and completion.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): The cause for ghusl: either ejaculation or full insertion with desire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does intercourse with a dead woman without ejaculation not require ghusl?
A) Because the act is haram.
B) Because the cause is incomplete.
C) Because it involves a dead body.
D) Because there is no desire.

Key Takeaway
Intercourse without ejaculation in contexts where ejaculation is not prevalent does not obligate ghusl, as the cause remains incomplete.

No Ghusl for Virgin with Intact Hymen and No Ejaculation
Ruling on contact with a virgin without breaking the hymen and without ejaculation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl after contact with a virgin without breaking the hymen and without ejaculation, and explain why the hymen prevents the obligation of ghusl.

Core Idea
Intact hymen prevents the meeting of circumcised parts, so no ghusl is required.

This lesson examines a specific case: a man has sexual contact with a virgin, but the hymen remains intact and no ejaculation occurs. According to the Hanafi school, this does not trigger the obligation of ghusl. The rationale lies in the condition for obligatory ghusl—the meeting of the circumcised parts—which the hymen obstructs. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi position on minimal requirements for sexual impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The passage describes 'hitting a virgin' (i.e., sexual contact) without removing the hymen and without ejaculation. The ruling is clear: no ghusl is required.
The term 'hitting' (إصابة) is a euphemism for sexual intercourse.
2
Trace the legal reasoning (talil)
The author provides the reason: 'because the hymen prevents the meeting of the two circumcised parts.' In Hanafi fiqh, the cause (sabab) for ghusl is the meeting of the circumcised parts (iltiqa' al-khatanayn). The hymen acts as a barrier preventing this meeting.
The two circumcised parts refer to the male glans and the female circumcision area (the female analogue is the area around the vaginal opening).
3
Recognize the condition for ghusl
The condition (shart) for ghusl to become obligatory is the meeting of the circumcised parts, even without ejaculation. In this case, the condition is absent because the hymen is intact.
The Hanafi school considers this condition a necessary cause; other schools may have different thresholds.
4
Understand the practical implication
If the hymen is not broken and no ejaculation occurs, there is no need to perform ghusl. The person remains in a state of purity and may continue acts of worship.
This ruling applies regardless of any other factors like pleasure or arousal.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require ghusl for any penetration, even if the hymen remains intact. The Hanafi position is more specific, conditioning the obligation on the actual meeting of the circumcised parts.
This is a point of legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإصابة بكر لم تُزِلِ بَكَارَتَهَا، مِنْ غير إنزال. سببه، وهو: الإيلاج مقام الإنزال، (و) منها: ( إصابة بكر، لم تُزلِ) الإصابة (بَكَارَتَها، مـن غـيـر إنزال)، لأن البكارة تمنع التقاء الختانين كذا في ( البزازية ) وغيرها.
And hitting a virgin without removing her virginity, without ejaculation. Its cause is that insertion stands in place of ejaculation. (And) from them: (Hitting a virgin, without the hit removing her virginity, without ejaculation), because the hymen prevents the meeting of the two circumcised parts, as stated in al-Bazzaziyya and others.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage covers the ninth item: sexual contact with a virgin where the hymen is not broken and there is no ejaculation. The reasoning is that the hymen prevents the meeting of the two circumcised parts (circumcised glans and circumcised area of the woman), which is a condition for ghusl according to some Hanafi views. Since the hymen is intact, it is as if full insertion has not occurred; therefore, no ghusl is required. References to al-Bazzaziyya and other works support this.

Key Terms
البكارة (al-bakārah): The hymen; a membrane that blocks full vaginal entry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man has contact with a virgin without breaking the hymen and without ejaculation, does ghusl become obligatory?
A) Yes, because there was sexual contact.
B) No, because the hymen prevents the meeting of circumcised parts.
C) Only if the woman feels pleasure.
D) Only if the man inserts his finger.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, ghusl is only obligatory when the circumcised parts meet; an intact hymen prevents this meeting, so no ghusl is required without ejaculation.

Ghusl and Semen Entry: Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on a woman who is touched without full intercourse and semen enters her vagina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for a woman when semen enters her vagina and explain the condition that triggers obligation.

Core Idea
Ghusl is tied to one's own ejaculation, not the presence of foreign semen.

This passage refines the general rule of ghusl after intercourse by addressing a specific scenario: partial contact with semen entry but no full intercourse. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the woman's own sexual discharge and that of her partner, and the ruling depends on evidence of her own ejaculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is partial intercourse (below the private part) with semen entering the vagina. The ruling: ghusl is not obligatory for the woman unless she becomes pregnant.
Focus on the condition 'unless she becomes pregnant'.
2
Understand the reason (talil)
Pregnancy is evidence of her own ejaculation (inzal), which is the actual trigger for ghusl. Without her own ejaculation, ghusl is not required.
The text explicitly states: 'because pregnancy is evidence of her ejaculation'.
3
Apply the principle to a related scenario
If she already performed ghusl, then later discharges semen that is not her own, no further ghusl is required; it is like urine (just wash the area).
This reinforces that the key factor is the source of the discharge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جومعت فيما دون الفرج، ودخل المني فرجها، لا يلزمها الغسل ما لم تحبل؛ لأن الحبل دليل إنزالها، ولو اغتسلت بـعـد الجماع، ثم خرج منها المني، إن كان منيه لا يلزمها الغسل؛ لأن الخارج إذا لم يكن مني المرأة، كان بمنزلة البول.
And if she is touched below the private part, and semen enters her vagina, ghusl is not obligatory upon her unless she becomes pregnant; because pregnancy is evidence of her ejaculation. And if she performed ghusl after intercourse, then semen came out from her, if it is his (the man's) semen, ghusl is not obligatory upon her; because when the discharge is not her own semen, it is like urine.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage provides additional rulings beyond the ten items. It deals with a scenario where a woman is touched externally (without full vaginal intercourse) and the man's semen enters her vagina. In this case, the woman does not need to perform ghusl unless she becomes pregnant, because pregnancy proves that she herself ejaculated (released her own seminal fluid) which would trigger ghusl. Also, if a woman has already performed ghusl after intercourse, and later she discharges what appears to be semen, if it is the man's semen (not her own), she does not need to repeat ghusl because it is considered impure like urine. This is a nuanced ruling that distinguishes between her own sexual fluid and that of her partner.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniy): Semen; the male reproductive fluid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does a woman become required to perform ghusl after partial intercourse where semen enters her vagina?
A) Immediately after the semen enters.
B) Only if she becomes pregnant.
C) If she feels arousal.
D) If the semen is her husband's.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a woman's obligation to perform ghusl is triggered by her own ejaculation, not by the mere presence of a man's semen.

When Ghusl Is Not Required: Conditions and Exceptions
Sets conditions for when ghusl is not required after intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where ghusl is not obligatory according to the Hanafi school and explain the rationale for each.

Core Idea
Ghusl is not required if the hymen remains intact without orgasm, if sperm enters externally without intercourse unless pregnancy, or if exiting sperm is not one's own.

This lesson examines specific scenarios where the general obligation of ghusl after sexual contact is lifted. Understanding these exceptions prevents unnecessary burden and reflects the school's attention to precise causes (asbab) of ritual impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
Determine what ruling is being discussed: the absence of obligation for ghusl in specific situations.
The general rule is that ghusl is required after intercourse or orgasm.
2
Examine the first case: virgin with intact hymen, no orgasm
Read the passage: intercourse with a virgin without breaking the hymen and without orgasm does not require ghusl. The rationale is that the hymen prevents the meeting of the circumcised parts.
The cause (sabab) of ghusl is the meeting of the two circumcised parts; here it is absent.
3
Examine the second case: external fondling with sperm entry
If sperm enters the vagina from external contact (not intercourse) and no orgasm occurs, ghusl is not required unless she becomes pregnant, which indicates she had an orgasm.
Pregnancy serves as evidence (qarinah) of orgasm.
4
Examine the third case: exiting husband's sperm after ghusl
If after performing ghusl, a woman finds her husband's sperm exiting, she does not need to repeat ghusl because it is not her own substance and is treated like urine.
The substance is considered najasah (impurity) but not a cause for ghusl.
5
Apply the rulings to new scenarios
Use the same principles to answer similar questions, such as what if the hymen is broken but no orgasm, or what if the exiting sperm is the woman's own.
The key is identifying the presence or absence of the cause of ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإصابة بكر لم تُزِلِ بَكَارَتَهَا، مِنْ غير إنزال. سببه، وهو: الإيلاج مقام الإنزال، (و) منها: ( إصابة بكر، لم تُزلِ) الإصابة (بَكَارَتَها، مـن غـيـر إنزال)، لأن البكارة تمنع التقاء الختانين كذا في ( البزازية ) وغيرها. ولو جومعت فيما دون الفرج، ودخل المني فرجها، لا يلزمها الغسل ما لم تحبل؛ لأن الحبل دليل إنزالها، ولو اغتسلت بـعـد الجماع، ثم خرج منها المني، إن كان منيه لا يلزمها الغسل؛ لأن الخارج إذا لم يكن مني المرأة، كان بمنزلة البول.
And injury to a virgin that did not break her virginity, without orgasm. Its cause, which is: insertion in place of orgasm. And among them: injury to a virgin, the injury did not break her virginity, without orgasm, because the hymen prevents the meeting of the two circumcised parts. So it is in al-Bazzaziyya and others. And if she is fondled in what is below the private part, and maniy enters her vagina, ghusl is not incumbent upon her unless she becomes pregnant; because pregnancy is evidence of her orgasm. And if she performed ghusl after intercourse, then maniy came out from her: if it is his maniy, ghusl is not incumbent upon her; because what exits, if it is not the woman's maniy, is like urine.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on rulings regarding the obligation of ghusl. The first ruling: if a virgin is involved in intercourse but her hymen is not broken and there is no orgasm, ghusl is not required. The reason is that the hymen prevents the meeting of the circumcised parts, meaning no actual contact of the genitals. The second ruling: if a woman is fondled externally and the man's sperm enters her vagina without intercourse, she does not need to perform ghusl unless she becomes pregnant, because pregnancy indicates she had an orgasm. The third ruling: if she performed ghusl after intercourse, and then after ghusl some of her husband's sperm exits from her, she does not need to repeat ghusl because the exiting substance is not her own but like urine.

Key Terms
بَكَارَة (bakaara): The hymen, a membrane that prevents the meeting of circumcised parts during intercourse.
إنزال (inzaal): Orgasm with ejaculation; the emission of maniy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman's husband's semen exits from her after she performed ghusl, does she need to repeat ghusl?
A) Yes, because semen is impure.
B) No, because it is not her own semen and is like urine.
C) Yes, because she had intercourse.
D) No, but she must wash that area only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school precisely defines when ghusl is obligatory based on the cause of ritual impurity; exceptions are made where that cause is absent or the substance is not one's own.

The Obligatory Acts of Ghusl and the Dispute over Mouth and Nose
Lists and proves the obligatory acts of ghusl with a focus on the disputed mouth-nose obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eleven obligatory acts of ghusl, explain that they reduce to one principle, and apply the Hanafi proof for the obligation of washing the mouth and nose.

Core Idea
The eleven faraid of ghusl all reduce to one: wash the entire body without hardship.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the obligatory components (faraid) of ghusl. After clarifying why the topic follows wudu, the author enumerates eleven acts but immediately reveals they reduce to a single principle: covering the body with water. Special attention is given to washing the mouth and nose, which is a debated obligation between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the enumeration and reduction
The text states there are eleven faraid but says they all 'return to one thing'. Identify that one thing as 'عموم الماء ما أمكن من الجسد بلا حرج' (general application of water to the body as much as possible without hardship).
The enumeration is for facilitation, not to suggest independent obligations.
2
Recognize the role of ijtihad
Note that washing the mouth and nose is called a 'فرض اجتهادي' (fard ijtihadi) – an obligation derived through reasoning, not explicit text. This explains why there is a difference of opinion.
The term indicates that the proof is not explicit but derived.
3
Trace the Hanafi proofs
Locate the Quranic verse (5:6) 'فاطهروا' – interpreted as washing the whole body, which includes internal parts like mouth and nose. Also note the hadith 'تحت كل شعرة جنابة' (under every hair is janabah) and the severe warning for leaving a hair unwashed.
The verse uses an intensive form (صيغة مبالغة) emphasizing thoroughness.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Understand that Imam al-Shafi'i considers mouth and nose as sunnah in ghusl, based on them being part of fitra. The Hanafi response: fitra (religion) is broader and does not negate obligation. Also, the wudu analogy fails because the face in wudu is only the exterior.
The dispute is a legitimate ikhtilaf (mukhtalaf fih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل لبيان فرائض الغسل ذكره بعد الوضوء اقتداء بالكتاب العزيز (۱) لأن الحاجة إلى الوضوء أكثر، أو لأن محله جزء البدن، ومحل الغسل كله، والجزء قبل الكل، ولأنه يسن تقديم الوضوء على الغسل. (يُفترض في الاغتسال من الجنابة، والحيض والنفاس ( أحدَ عَشَرَ شيئاً)، وكلها ترجع إلى شيء واحد، هو : عموم الماء ما أمكن من الجسد بلا حرج، ولكن ذكر التي عدها تسهيلاً، وإيقاظـاً لـمـا يـلــزم غسله. منها: (غَسْلُ الفم والأنف) (۳)، وهو فرض اجتهادي، لاختلاف العلماء، لقول الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله بسنية غسلهما، ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُمْ جُنُبا فَاطَهَرُوا﴾ [المائدة: ٦] أي: فاغسلوا أبدانكم، والبدن يتناول الظاهر والباطن، وما فيه حرج سقط للضرورة، وهمـا يـغـســلان عــادة وعبادة، نـفـلاً في الوضوء، وفرضاً في النجاسة الحقيقية، فشملهما نص الكتاب، وهو صيغة مبالغة، وقوله : ( تَحْتَ كُلِّ شَعْرَةٍ جَنَابَةٌ، فَبُلُوا الشَّعْرَ وَأَنْقُوا الْبَشَرَةَ ) رواه الترمذي من غير معارض. وقوله : « مَنْ تَرَكَ شَعْرَةٌ مِنْ جَسَدِهِ لَمْ يَغْسِلُها فُعل بِهِ كَذَا وَكَذَا مِنَ النَّارِ » قال علي : « فَمِن ثُمَّ عادِيتُ شَعرِي وكَانَ يجزه» (). وكونهما من الفطرة لا ينفي الوجوب، لأنها الدين، وهو أعم منه، فلا يعارضه بخلافهما في الوضوء، لأن الوجه هو ما يقع به المواجهة، ولا تكون بداخل الأنف والفم، (والبدن) عطـف عـام على خاص، وقدمنا () الإشارة إلى اشتراط زوال ما يمنع وصول الماء إلى الجسد، وعمــوم الاستيعاب، فلو شرب الماء عباً مستوعباً الفم أجزأ، والأفضل إلقاء الماء؛ لكونه مستعملاً فيكــره
Section explaining the obligatory acts of ghusl. He mentioned it after wudu, following the noble Book, because the need for wudu is greater, or because its place is part of the body, while the place of ghusl is the whole body, and the part comes before the whole, and because it is recommended to precede ghusl with wudu. It is obligatory in bathing from janabah, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding (eleven things), and all of them return to one thing: general application of water to the body as much as possible without hardship. But he enumerated them for facilitation and to alert to what must be washed. Among them: washing the mouth and nose (3), and it is an obligatory by ijtihad, due to scholars' disagreement. Imam al-Shafi'i said they are sunnah. Our evidence: Allah's saying {And if you are in a state of janabah, then purify yourselves} (5:6), meaning wash your bodies. The body includes apparent and internal parts, and what has hardship is dropped out of necessity. They are washed customarily and devotionally, as supererogatory in wudu and obligatory in real impurity. So the text of the Book includes them; it is an intensive form. And his saying: 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin' – reported by Tirmidhi without opposition. And his saying: 'Whoever leaves a hair on his body unwashed, such and such from the Fire will be done to him.' Ali said: 'From then on I was hostile to my hair and he used to cut it.' And their being from the fitra does not negate obligation, because fitra is the religion, which is more general than it; so it does not contradict. Unlike them in wudu, because the face is what meets others, and it does not include the interior of nose and mouth. (And the body) is a general conjunction to the specific. And we previously indicated the condition of removing what prevents water reaching the body, and the general encompassing. So if one drinks water swallowing it, encompassing the mouth, it suffices, and the better is to toss out water because it becomes used water and becomes disliked.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section of obligatory acts of ghusl. The author first explains why the topic is placed after wudu: because the need for wudu is more frequent, and because the body part washed in wudu is a part of the whole body of ghusl, and the part precedes the whole. Also, it is sunnah to perform wudu before ghusl. Then he states that in ghusl from janabah, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding, there are eleven obligatory acts, but they all reduce to one principle: pouring water over the entire body without hardship. The enumeration is for ease. Among these are washing the mouth and nose. This is a debated issue: Imam al-Shafi'i considers them sunnah, but the Hanafis hold them as obligatory. Their proof includes the Quranic verse 'purify yourselves' which implies the entire body, and the hadith 'under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin' (Tirmidhi). The hadith mentioning punishment for leaving a hair unwashed is also cited. The fact that mouth and nose are part of the fitra does not negate obligation. The analogy with wudu is rejected because in wudu, the face is only the exterior. The author also notes the condition of removing barriers to water and the requirement of general coverage. If one drinks water such that it fills the mouth, that suffices, but the preferred method is to expel the water because water becomes used and using it is disliked.

Key Terms
فرض اجتهادي (fard ijtihadi): An obligation derived through scholarly reasoning (ijtihad), not explicit text; hence debated.
رجعة (ruju'): Not applicable here. The text uses 'ترجع إلى' meaning 'return to' or 'reduce to'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis consider washing the mouth and nose obligatory in ghusl despite them being part of fitra?
A) Because the Quran explicitly commands it.
B) Because they are included in the command to wash the body, and fitra does not negate obligation.
C) Because they are required for wudu.
D) Because the Prophet always did it.

Key Takeaway
The eleven faraid of ghusl all reduce to washing the whole body; the mouth and nose are included as wajib in the Hanafi school based on textual evidence and ijtihad.

Taharah — Purification

The Obligatory Acts of Ghusl: Eleven Faraid and the Hanafi Proof for Rinsing Mouth and Nose
Shifting from listing faraid to defending the obligation of mouth and nose rinsing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eleven obligatory acts of ghusl in the Hanafi school and explain the textual evidence that establishes rinsing the mouth and nose as fard.

Core Idea
Rinsing the mouth and nose is fard in ghusl because the Quranic command 'fa'taharu' covers the entire body, including its accessible interiors.

In this lesson, we examine the faraid (obligatory acts) of ghusl from the Hanafi perspective, focusing on the disputed ruling regarding rinsing the mouth and nose. The author of Imdad al-Fattah presents the textual evidence that establishes these acts as fard, and responds to the Shafi'i position that considers them sunnah. Understanding this dispute clarifies the essential components of a valid ghusl and the role of evidence in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eleven faraid of ghusl
The passage states that there are eleven obligatory acts in ghusl, but explains that they all reduce to one: covering the entire body with water without hardship. The listing includes washing the mouth, nose, and the entire body. Recognize that the mouth and nose are specifically highlighted as part of the fard.
The eleven acts are enumerated for ease, but the essence is complete coverage of the body.
2
Locate the inter-madhhab dispute
The text notes that Imam al-Shafi'i considers rinsing the mouth and nose as sunnah in ghusl, not fard. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of the evidence. Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): if these acts are omitted, the ghusl is valid in the Shafi'i school but invalid in the Hanafi school.
The dispute is not about the acts themselves but their legal status.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
The Hanafi position is supported by three key proofs: (1) Quran 5:6 'fa'taharu' interpreted as washing the body, which includes interior parts that are customarily washed; (2) the hadith 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and cleanse the skin' (reported by al-Tirmidhi); (3) the statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he shaved his hair out of fear of leaving a hair unwashed. These establish that the obligation extends to the interior accessible parts.
The hyperbolical form 'fa'taharu' emphasizes complete purification.
4
Examine the rebuttal of the Shafi'i argument
The Shafi'i argument that rinsing the mouth and nose are from fitrah and thus not obligatory is rebutted: fitrah (din) is a broader concept that includes both obligatory and recommended acts. Being from fitrah does not negate obligation. Additionally, the difference from wudu (where these acts are sunnah) is explained: the face in wudu is defined as the external surface that is encountered (yuwajahu biha), not the interior.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'face' in wudu and 'body' in ghusl.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Consider a case where someone performs ghusl but only runs water over the outer body, neglecting the mouth and nose. According to the Hanafi school, the ghusl is incomplete and must be repeated because a fard act was omitted. In contrast, the Shafi'i school would deem the ghusl valid. This difference has real implications for the validity of worship.
The obligation of rinsing the mouth and nose in ghusl is a distinctive Hanafi ruling.
